{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:06","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:06","title":"Some college students are set to head home for Thanksgiving — and may bring COVID with them. Here the impact it may have — and how to mitigate it.","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/students-going-home-thanksgiving-might-bring-covid-how-to-stop-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f722e510ab50d00184acd52?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Empty Plane coronavirus pandemic airplane\" height=\"2903\" width=\"3871\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">John Minchillo/AP</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>With COVID cases on the rise, especially in college towns, families may need to change Thanksgiving plans.</li>\n<li>Risky behaviors out of class and changing weather may also lead to more cases among students.</li>\n<li>Universities and towns can begin preparing now by putting in place mitigation measures, and students should alter their plans to incorporate as many safety measures as possible.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Dr. Anthony Fauci, the nation's leading infectious disease expert, warned this week that families may need to <a href=\"https://www.cbsnews.com/news/fauci-thanksgiving-covid-different/\">change their Thanksgiving plans</a> to keep everyone safe from the coronavirus. The head of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, Robert Redfield, <a href=\"https://www.cnn.com/2020/10/14/politics/white-house-task-force-small-gatherings/index.html\">expressed similar concerns</a> in a call with governors.</p><p>There has been an <a href=\"https://covid.cdc.gov/covid-data-tracker/#cases_casesinlast7days\">alarming increase in COVID-19 cases</a> in most states in recent weeks, and we have seen cases rise <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2020/us/covid-college-cases-tracker.html\">in college towns</a> in particular. Colder weather means more activities are moving indoors, where <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/when-covid-19-superspreaders-are-talking-where-you-sit-in-the-room-matters-145966\">the virus can circulate</a>. And people who have been socially isolated for months feel <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/coronavirus/2019-ncov/daily-life-coping/managing-stress-anxiety.html\">desperate for connection</a>.</p><p>As the holidays approach, one important question is what impact sending college students home for Thanksgiving will have on their home communities.</p><p>The Public Health Response Team at Texas A&#38;M University, which <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/citations?user=3nelzqkAAAAJ&hl=enl=en\">the three</a> <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/citations?user=gdcVdXwAAAAJ&hl=enl=en\">of us</a> <a href=\"https://temag.tamu.edu/directory/walter-t-casey/\">serve on</a>, has been documenting COVID-19 trends in Texas for the past six months and forecasting disease spread and the impacts on hospitals. With new cases ticking upward, we have been concerned about what the holidays will bring.</p><p><iframe width=\"100%\" height=\"400px\" frameborder=\"0\" src=\"https://datawrapper.dwcdn.net/F9zYa/1/\"></iframe></p><h2>Dual hot spots</h2><p>In Texas, the most densely populated counties have a higher proportion of documented SARS-CoV-2 infections, and they contain a majority of the state's colleges and universities. The intersection of these features creates challenges for controlling COVID-19.</p><p>Many of these counties were already COVID-19 hot spots before students returned for the fall semester. In more crowded communities, the chance of random exposure to someone with COVID-19 is a lot less random and a lot more certain.</p><p>Reopening the campuses brought in an <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/volumes/69/wr/mm6939e4.htm?s_cid=mm6939e4_w\">age demographic</a> known to harbor the virus but often with only mild symptoms or no symptoms at all. This facilitated covert spread of the virus. Nationwide, many universities reported a <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/volumes/69/wr/mm6939e4.htm?s_cid=mm6939e4_w\">surge in cases</a> shortly after students returned in the fall, and some had to stop in-person classes and <a href=\"https://abc11.com/unc-covid-nc-coronavirus/7029408/\">shift back to online learning</a> when those cases got out of control.</p><p>Soon, these students will be returning to their families around the country for the holidays, and bringing with them the possibility of a souvenir no one wants — COVID-19.</p><p><iframe width=\"100%\" height=\"400px\" frameborder=\"0\" src=\"https://datawrapper.dwcdn.net/O9Wjv/12/\"></iframe></p><h2>Cause for concern</h2><p>It's not just campus activities that raise concerns. It's also what students do when they aren't in class, including going to bars and attending off-campus parties.</p><p>In Texas, Gov. Greg Abbott recently relaxed the minimal restrictions for bars, allowing them to open at 50% capacity. This likely means that bars — chafing from being treated differently from other places where people gather in groups — will be rarin' to go. With young adults, the likelihood of <a href=\"https://www.insidehighered.com/news/2020/03/11/student-activities-risk-spread-coronavirus\">failing to remain vigilant</a> about social distancing, mask-wearing and other precautions is already a concern. Such risky behaviors would compound the risk factors, facilitating extra opportunities for the <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/mmwr/volumes/69/wr/mm6936a5.htm\">virus to spread</a>.</p><p>We hypothesize that factors such as <a href=\"https://www.jahonline.org/article/S1054-139X(20)30489-4/pdf\">college drinking</a>, <a href=\"https://www.uwhealth.org/news/staying-safe-and-managing-social-pressures-during-the-pandemic-w/53464\">peer and social pressure</a> to act as if everything is normal, as well as <a href=\"https://www.technologyreview.com/2020/10/08/1009650/winter-will-make-the-pandemic-worse/\">seasonal changes</a> that make outdoor eating and drinking less feasible, will increase the likelihood of exposure to the coronavirus and subsequent infection.</p><p>As these young adults return home, some will probably have signs of mild illness. Some may have no discernible symptoms but will still be infectious. They could introduce the virus to communities that have had few infections so far and to the friends and family, including <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/coronavirus/2019-ncov/need-extra-precautions/older-adults.html\">vulnerable parents and grandparents</a>, they have been eager to reconnect with.</p><figure></figure><p>While we have been studying the dynamics of COVID-19's spread in Texas specifically, many other states with large college populations face the same combination of factors, with potentially infectious young adults heading home.</p><h2>So what can be done now?</h2><p>First, university and town officials can <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/coronavirus/2019-ncov/community/colleges-universities/considerations.html\">prepare for these upcoming risks</a>. Efforts to contain and mitigate the spread of SARS-CoV-2 on campuses and in surrounding communities are as important as ever.</p><p>Some campuses are planning to <a href=\"https://www.chronicle.com/article/fearing-a-second-wave-of-covid-19-some-colleges-will-end-fall-semester-early\">end the semester at Thanksgiving</a> break or shift to online classes for the final weeks to avoid additional travel that could spread the virus. Others have <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/local/education/welcome-to-college-now-get-tested-for-the-coronavirus--again-and-again/2020/09/04/2d087722-ed2f-11ea-b4bc-3a2098fc73d4_story.html\">implemented rigorous testing and contact tracing programs</a> to help stop the spread.</p><p>Second, everyone needs to take COVID-19 public health preventive practices seriously. That means avoiding large gatherings — and even smaller ones when new people are involved — wearing face masks, and following the recommended physical distancing guidelines. When going to bars or restaurants, be aware of <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/coronavirus/2019-ncov/community/organizations/business-employers/bars-restaurants.html\">how well the customers and the businesses take precautions</a>.</p><p>Third, everyone should realize the upcoming holiday season will be different from those in years past. Students whose semester won't end until December should consider avoiding travel until the end of the year — or, if they are traveling earlier, <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/fleeing-from-the-coronavirus-is-dangerous-for-you-the-people-you-encounter-along-the-way-and-wherever-you-end-up-133995\">to do it safely</a>. One strategy is to get tested before heading home and again before returning. If you aren't able to visit your grandparents and other family members in person, you can find remote ways to still connect, such as phone calls or video chats.</p><p>We wish everyone a good and safe holiday season — keeping yourself and others safe will mean knowing what the risks are and proactively taking steps to ensure many more happy family holidays to come.<!-- Below is The Conversation's page counter tag. 23:59:05","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","title":"Warren Buffett and Joe Biden are bullish on America, agree the super rich should pay higher taxes, and want quarterly earnings guidance to end","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/warren-buffett-joe-biden-share-views-america-taxes-earnings-guidance-2020-10","description":"<p><strong><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f8d83df9e9fe4001900a01e?format=jpeg\" alt=\"berkshire hathaway warren buffett\" height=\"709\" width=\"1419\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Rick Wilking/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></strong></p><ul><li><strong>Joe Biden and Warren Buffett share the view that America has unrivaled potential to be a global leader.</strong></li><li><strong>\"Just got off the phone with Warren Buffett, talking about how we have position unlike 50, 70, 80 years ago to lead the whole damn world in a way that no one else can,\" the Democratic presidential candidate said last week.</strong></li><li><strong>The former vice president and the billionaire investor also agree on the need for higher taxes on the super rich, and that quarterly earnings guidance should be scrapped.</strong></li><li><strong>\"Warren Buffett said it best when he stated that he should not pay a lower tax rate than his secretary,\" Biden's campaign website reads.</strong></li><li><strong><strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/international?r=US&IR=TIR=T\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</strong></strong><hr></li></ul><p>Joe Biden spoke to Warren Buffett about America's revitalized potential to be a global leader <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-joe-biden-presidential-election-america-global-leadership-2020-10-1029682841#\">ahead of a virtual fundraiser last week</a>, marking the first reported conversation between the two men this year.</p><p>The Democratic presidential candidate and the billionaire investor have similar views on several subjects. Here's a look at the topics they agree on:</p><h2>1) The US has bright prospects</h2><p>Biden and Buffett are both optimistic about America's future influence and prosperity.</p><p>\"Just got off the phone with Warren Buffett, talking about how we have position unlike 50, 70, 80 years ago to lead the whole damn world in a way that no one else can,\" the former vice president said last week.</p><p>Biden's comment echoed Buffett's view at <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-berkshire-hathaway-2020-annual-meeting-highlights-2020-5-1029156864#?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\">Berkshire Hathaway's annual meeting in May</a>. \"Nothing can stop America when you get right down to it,\" the Berkshire CEO said.</p><p><strong data-stringify-type=\"bold\"><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\">Read More:</em></strong><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\">&#160;</em><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-crash-3-nonsensical-narratives-dangerous-heights-peter-cecchini-2020-10\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-stringify-link=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-crash-3-nonsensical-narratives-dangerous-heights-peter-cecchini-2020-10\" data-sk=\"tooltip_parent\">An ex-Wall Street chief strategist lambasts 3 'nonsensical narratives' he says are pushing stocks to dangerous heights — and warns that the current rally is unsustainable</a></em></p><h2>2) The super rich should pay higher taxes</h2><p>Biden and Buffett agree that the wealthiest people in America should pay higher taxes.</p><p>As vice president to President Barack Obama, Biden unsuccessfully pushed for Congress to pass the \"Buffett rule\" to ensure those earning more than $1 million a year would pay an effective tax rate of at least 30%.</p><p>The proposal was <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-bob-woodward-microsoft-bill-gates-37-billion-deal-2020-10-1029667401#\">named after Buffett</a> because the investor <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-higher-taxes-class-warfare-extraordinary-kurt-andersen-2020-10-1029656423\">famously observed</a> that he pays a higher tax rate than his secretary.</p><p>\"Warren Buffett, who many people in America have come to know as an extremely successful, generous, and gregarious man, shined a very, very bright light on this subject,\" Biden <a href=\"https://obamawhitehouse.archives.gov/the-press-office/2012/04/12/remarks-vice-president-joe-biden-tax-fairness-campaign-event\">said at a campaign event</a> in New Hampshire in April 2012.</p><p>\"He noted the absurd fact that he as a billionaire, under the current tax laws, pays a lower tax rate than his secretary pays,\" the politician continued.</p><p><strong data-stringify-type=\"bold\"><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\">Read More:&#160;</em></strong><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-picks-to-buy-small-companies-recommendations-high-upside-rbc-2020-10\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-stringify-link=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-picks-to-buy-small-companies-recommendations-high-upside-rbc-2020-10\" data-sk=\"tooltip_parent\">Buy these 7 unheralded stocks right now for near-term upside of at least 25% as growth accelerates to a new level, RBC says</a></em></p><p>Biden called for a change to that situation in a tweet at the time.</p><p>\"I'm for the Buffett Rule because it just makes sense,\" he said. \"Like the President says—it's not class warfare. It's math.\"</p><p>Biden plans to pursue a similar policy if he becomes president. Part of his plan to expand access to affordable healthcare is to eliminate capital-gains tax loopholes and roll back President Trump's tax cut on the super wealthy, which would double the capital-gains tax rate to nearly 40% for those making over $1 million a year.</p><p>\"Warren Buffett said it best when he stated that he should not pay a lower tax rate than his secretary,\" <a href=\"https://joebiden.com/healthcare/#\">Biden's campaign website reads</a>.</p><h2>3) Corporations are too focused on the short term</h2><p>Biden and Buffett have both flagged the problem of companies prioritizing short-term gains over long-term progress.</p><p>Economic growth and wage gains suffer when corporations pump up their stock prices instead of investing for the long run, Biden argued in a <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-short-termism-saps-the-economy-1475018087\">Wall Street Journal op-ed in September 2016</a>.</p><p>Linking executive compensation to stock prices, unlimited buybacks, tax laws that treat investments as long term after one year, activist investors pushing for near-term gains, and a \"financial culture focused on quarterly earnings and short-run metrics\" all contribute to the issue, he said.</p><p>Buffett and JPMorgan CEO Jamie Dimon called for public companies to consider ditching quarterly earnings guidance in their own <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/short-termism-is-harming-the-economy-1528336801?emailToken=7cfbd7d1034a87693b3b3d2b3ae41bf3KAhJzw3s88ciwQxj82kNl/GWEzmmvMNQPrNjYc/CX6rNP5vtShhZ2oQC4YXFm9O0azse5mOiv1Ixlbcuno4RTQ%3D%3D&reflink=article_copyURL_sharehare\">Wall Street Journal op-ed in June 2018</a>. The two executives argued it encourages companies to sacrifice long-term progress and sustainability for short-term profits.</p><p>\"Jamie Dimon &#38; Warren Buffett's call to end quarterly guidance is an important step toward ending our nation's culture of short-termism,\" Biden <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JoeBiden/status/1005469583904530432\">tweeted in response</a>.</p><p>\"America's workers &#38; investors will all be better off with a system that rewards those who build for the long-term,\" he added.</p><h2>A quiet alliance?</h2><p>Biden shares Buffett's positive view of America, his desire to raise taxes on the ultra wealthy, and his belief that a short-term focus hurts companies in the long run.</p><p>Buffett hasn't publicly endorsed Biden for president, but given their agreement on several subjects, it's easy to imagine the pair working together if the Democrat is elected.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a (Theron Mohamed)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f8d83df9e9fe4001900a01e?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn68@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-46-722_29a2e7886431b622.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn56@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-47-418_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=lY7bv9Ilz2s:DHiSGfxinEo:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn60@2020_3/2020/10/19/15-59-43-311_3a88668a1b2237f8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=lY7bv9Ilz2s:DHiSGfxinEo:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn63@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-48-279_3b6ffe1ecd53d567.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn91@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-43-999_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=lY7bv9Ilz2s:DHiSGfxinEo:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn39@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-39-099_1e02c024fa54394b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/lY7bv9Ilz2s":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn51@2020_2/2020/10/19/15-59-44-407_459494902c1031c8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1603123145988},{"createdTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:06","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:06","title":"I drove a $49,000 Acura TLX to see if all-new luxury sedan can still deliver in a world dominated by SUVs","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/review-acura-tlx-luxury-sedan-specs-photos-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f847e1b642e9400180b55b0?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The 2021 Acura TLX has a dynamic exterior.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>I tested a $49,325 <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/category/acura\">Acura</a> TLX sedan, in Advance trim with all-wheel-drive.</li>\n<li>The TLX is all-new for the 2021 model year.</li>\n<li>My tester had a potent, 272-horsepower four-cylinder turbocharged engine under the hood.</li>\n<li>The Acura TLX is in a tough position as SUVs take over the world, but this sedan has always been a stupendous bargain, and the new edition is no exception.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>The Acura TLX is a classic example of what we used to call a \"fine automobile.\"&#160;</p><p>It's the sort of well-made, conservatively-styled sedan that can be sporty, but doesn't have to be. When you throw in parent Honda's impeccable engineering and legendary reliability, the result is something quite fine, indeed.</p><p>Maybe too fine for its own good. I'm not sure you can find a better luxury midsize four-door — and I'm including traditional rivals such as the BMW 3-Series and the Audi A6. I lean German all the time, but whenever I come off some seat time in the Ohio-made Acura, I lean back toward the TLX. And when it comes to the A-Spec special editions, I've been ready to open my checkbook.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f85c264c255d800190b994f?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"700\" width=\"933\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The TLX nameplate has been around since the 2015 model year.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The TLX is all-new for the 2021 model year, born into a world increasingly dominated by SUVs.&#160;Acura sells those, too, but like all luxury marques, the big question these days is, \"Should we stick with the four-doors?\"</p><p>In Honda/Acura's case, the answer is clearly, \"Yes,\" and most industry observers think that the carmaker and historic foe Toyota/Lexus can continue to make sedans work. But they need to be at the tip-top of their game.</p><p>I got to see how tip-top when I sampled $49,325 TLX SH-AWD (\"super-handling all-wheel-drive\") Advance. The car started at a well-loaded $48,300 and got no extras, just an additional grand tacked on as a destination and handling fee. (The base TLX is $37,500.)</p><h2>Staggering value for a luxury four-door</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f85c28cc255d800190b9950?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1239\" width=\"1652\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The design is basically conservative, but with a lot of edges and angles.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>This is absolutely staggering value for a premium four-door, but then again, the TLX has offered staggering value for five years.</p><p>The TLX worked its TLX-y magic on me in less than an hour, causing me to question my allegiance to a small personal fleet of Toyota hybrids. I nearly took the plunge on a TSX, a TLX predecessor, when I lived on the West Coast, and so every time I get behind the wheel, I endure a bout of what-if lamentations. I know I said \"fine automobile,\" but for sedan connoisseurs, there might be no finer set of wheels.&#160;</p><p>If you think the TLX is a tricked-up Accord, think again. The new car has been uniquely designed, and the overall build quality is stupendous.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f848ec1c255d800190b9539?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The Acura TLX is competing in a segment that&#39;s been losing out to SUVs.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>My tester arrived in a sleek Modern Steel Metallic paint job with a groovy baseball-glove brown interior, and the combo was fire. The gray exterior was ideal for accentuating the TLX's angles and curves, of which there are certainly more of the former than the latter. A set of 19-inch wheels initially struck me as too small, but over a week, I decided they were just right.</p><p>One does need to have a mind for edge-work to enjoy looking at the TLX, which seems as though it's less a single, fastback shape than a concatenation of voids, tangents, tucks, and bends. It borders on Cubism, but Acura has been refining this aesthetic for a while now, and it with the 2021 TLX, the concept has reached a pleasing apotheosis.</p><h2>A soothing interior that's still a bit cramped in the back</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f848ec5c255d800190b9541?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Truck space is adequate.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>With the TLX, the busy exterior is soothed by a more placid, though high-tech, interior. We don't think enough about seats until they start to make us uncomfortable, and the TLX's 16-way front, heated and cooled, absolutely do not.</p><p>But they aren't recliners on wheels, either, as the TLX is supposed to offer spirited motoring in Sport mode, when the car's rotary drive selector — borrowed from the NSX supercar — is toggled in that direction.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f847e1c642e9400180b55b3?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The drive selector is taken from the NSX supercar.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Rearward, the legroom is ... not great. My suddenly tall teenage son would not have relished bending his knees to ride back there. But the trunk offers 13.5 cubic feet of capacity, and the interior is kinda roomy overall, so there isn't much justification for stretching the TLX to deal with the needs of adult rear-seat passengers.</p><p>Under the hood, the TLX packs a 2.0-liter, turbocharged inline four-cylinder engine, making 272 horsepower with 280 pound-feet of torque. This sucker punches above its weight big time. Even with a 10-speed automatic transmission that one might think would race toward the overdrive gears to vindicate the 21 mpg city/ 29 highway/24 combined fuel-economy rating, the TLX feels both potent and crisp, with a punchy 0-60 mph time of around six seconds.&#160;</p><h2>Handles like a champ</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f848ec2c255d800190b953b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The interior is soothing.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The handling is variable. Sport Mode encourages a brisk mood swing that welcomes harder acceleration, but the electric steering got a little too heavy for my tastes.</p><p>I ended up exploring the Individual setting, which offered the best of both worlds and still maxed out the TLX's enthusiastic yet admirably capable suspension. Like every other TLX I've driven in the past few years, the stiffness equation was perfectly calculated: My spine was thankful and I didn't have to ease off during hard cornering.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f847e1c642e9400180b55b1?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The infotainment screen rises above the dash, into the driver&#39;s line of sight.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>As with its luxe four-door brethren, the TLX comes with a suite of driver-assist features, and they all work as advertised. But this is a car I like to drive myself, so I made limited use of them.</p><p>In fact, I drove the TLX like I've driven some recent BMW test cars, while with Audis, Mercedes, and Lexuses, I've deferred to the adaptive cruise control option. Paddle shifters provide a manual capability, but I skipped it for the most part, allowing the computers to do a better job than I could and savoring the sonically augmented exhaust tones.</p><p>The infotainment system is a bit of a thorny issue here. Gone is the dual-screen of the previous generation, and in its place, a large central screen, with features controlled using a double trackpad, plus some buttons and knob (for audio volume).</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f847e1d642e9400180b55b4?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The touchpad controller takes some getting used to.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Once you get the hang of it, the setup works quite well. But you have to get the hang of it. As far as the tech itself goes, all boxes are checked: Bluetooth pairing, USB device integration, GPS navigation, wireless charging.</p><p>My tester was outfitted with Acura's sublime ELS Studio 3D audio system, a 17-speaker rig that is among the best available in the industry — and it's standard on the top-level Advance trim. Apple CarPlay and Android Auto are available.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f847e1a642e9400180b55ad?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The TLX is a joy to drive, although the electric steering felt too taut at times.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>It should be rather obvious by now that I think the 2021 Acura TLX is a winner. But I already thought it was a winner, so more of the same, new generation.</p><p>The next question is, \"Did Acura remain tip-top with the car?\"</p><h2>Nothing missing, and the price is still very right</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f85c7e291aa9e001880b45a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Acura TLX\" height=\"796\" width=\"1061\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The TLX remains a superb value.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Matthew DeBord/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Acura missed nothing in improving the TLX, and even gave a new infotainment system the old college try. You have to admire this obsessive commitment to ongoing excellence at a price below $50,000. As good as a lot of BMWs, better than some, and a relative bargain.</p><p>Sedan sales have been sliding in the US, and to keep cars such as then TLX in the game, carmakers like Acura have been upping prices. The new TLX is pricier than the old vehicle, but only by a few thousand bucks. I would argue that the outgoing sedan was underpriced, so at the very least, the new TLX is fairly stickered.&#160;</p><p>But the truth is that this terrific car remains a great buy. If you think about it — or drive it — I suspect you'll agree.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a com/~ff/businessinsider?i=1CRfevFZA7g:oL0eaG94S2U:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn40@2020_4/2020/10/19/15-59-41-201_89daa67bab3b817b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=1CRfevFZA7g:oL0eaG94S2U:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn79@2020_3/2020/10/19/15-59-43-933_39485ed6e7f4d132.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn91@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-43-999_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=1CRfevFZA7g:oL0eaG94S2U:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn31@2020_6/2020/10/19/15-59-49-491_cc8a0740b88b48d0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/1CRfevFZA7g":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn3@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-46-343_066e78d8dbc27dfe.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1603123146061},{"createdTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","title":"Most bosses think they're doing a better job leading than they actually are. Here are some ways to close the gap.","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/employees-managers-pandemic-leadership-struggles-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f1739e22618b96dc474c7a4?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Zoom fashion\" height=\"1440\" width=\"1920\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Bosses now have to learn how to lead their teams from home.\n<p class=\"copyright\">staticnak1983 / getty images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>A recent survey from management software provider 15Five found that 84% of leaders feel they're doing a good job supporting employees during the pandemic.</li>\n<li>By comparison, only 69% of employees felt their bosses were doing a good job.</li>\n<li>This disconnect exists because leaders have to attend to people's emotional and work needs, and they may not have the tools in place to do so effectively.&#160;</li>\n<li>To create a better environment at work, bosses need to model a healthy work-life balance and be more empathetic.&#160;</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>It's a bad time to be a boss.&#160;</p><p>When the coronavirus forced US companies to go remote in March, most companies were not ready. Along with the logistical issues of moving a business remote, the shift to 24/7 WFH created an even bigger challenge for managers, who now had to lead their stressed-out and struggling teams through a computer screen.&#160;</p><p>Nearly eight months into the pandemic, things haven't gotten much better. And there's a clear disconnect between how managers <em>think </em>they're doing, and employees' true feelings about their bosses.&#160;</p><p>In a survey of 750 full-time US managers and 750 employees the performance management software company <a href=\"https://www.15five.com/data-study/2020-workplace-report/?utm_source=pr&utm_medium=release&utm_campaign=release=release\">15Five</a> found that 84% of leaders reported feeling that they were doing a good job supporting employees during the pandemic, but only 69% of employees feel the same way.</p><p>The results suggest the presence of what psychologists call an <a href=\"https://ethicsunwrapped.utexas.edu/glossary/overconfidence-bias\">\"overconfidence bias,\"</a> in which people hold false beliefs about their abilities relative to objective measures. In this case, bosses believed they were better at managing and supporting employees than they actually were.</p><p>David Hassell, CEO and cofounder of 15Five, said this disconnect exists because managers have to support workers' emotional and work needs, and many companies haven't given them the tools to do so properly. Some 65% of managers in the 15Five survey said it was more difficult than usual to do their job effectively; 76% of senior level management said the same.&#160;</p><p>\"Connection is more important than ever, but it's harder to develop in this environment, especially when the structure isn't there,\" Hassell told Business Insider in an email. \"One-on-one meetings between managers and direct reports are so important now because they build a strong foundation of trust and help deepen relationships.\"&#160;</p><p>The pandemic has affected nearly everyone's mental health. A <a href=\"https://news.prudential.com/presskits/prudentials-pulse-american-worker-survey-work-in-progress.htm\">report from Prudential</a> found that 65% of American workers say the pandemic has added a great deal of stress to their lives.&#160;</p><p>Remote work also makes it surprisingly easy to skip lunch and work longer hours. It blurs the divide between work and home, which can lead to burnout.&#160;</p><p>Bosses need to be empathetic to these challenges, Rana el Kaliouby, cofounder and CEO of the software company Affectiva, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/3-ways-to-be-a-more-empathetic-leader-during-pandemic-2020-5\">wrote for Inc.</a></p><p dir=\"ltr\">\"We're all going through this pandemic, but we're not all in the same boat,\" she wrote. \"This is impacting people in all different ways, and as leaders we can't overlook that.\"</p><p>Nani Vishwanath, people team manager at Limeade, an employee experience software company, previously told Business Insider bosses need to encourage <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/paid-time-off-policies-employee-burnout-4-day-work-week-2020-8\">a healthy work-life balance</a> to ensure their employees aren't burning out. A few ways of doing this include: encouraging employees to take PTO, surveying your team, and having regular well-being check-ins.</p><p>\"When an employee finishes a demanding project or makes a big deadline, say 'You've been working really hard. Let's find a good time for you to take some time off to recharge,'\" Vishwanath told Business Insider's Marguerite Ward.&#160;</p><p>But the absolute best way to promote work-life balance is to model it yourself. If you tell employees to take their PTO, make sure you're taking it, too. Take regular breaks and encourage employees to do the same. Be understanding of workers challenges, but also be upfront when you're struggling yourself.&#160;</p><p>There's no such thing as a perfect boss and not every employee will love your management style — but there is a certain empathy that is required for leading during a time of crisis.&#160;</p><p>\"We are all leaders, be it at work, or in our families, or in our communities,\" el Kaliouby said. \"How we lead in these challenging times will have a ripple effect.\"</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/5f6bc442c4049200115cb495?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"92de258c01c17dcee502c6628d299c56","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn55@2020_2/2020/10/19/15-59-38-950_d218e5f4eed24295.webp","destWidth":1494,"destHeight":1121,"sourceBytes":83974,"destBytes":83974,"author":"chroncich@businessinsider.com Hroncich)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f1739e22618b96dc474c7a4?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn71@2020_6/2020/10/19/15-59-49-697_36aa72c77771b5b8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn56@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-47-418_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_Ew7bh_YMdc:0d2qI6zwGto:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn51@2020_2/2020/10/19/15-59-43-750_477814baeae7274e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_Ew7bh_YMdc:0d2qI6zwGto:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn19@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-48-576_e256d058f064cee1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn91@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-43-999_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_Ew7bh_YMdc:0d2qI6zwGto:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn56@2020_1/2020/10/19/15-59-46-243_ebcd6c3c09aec53d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/_Ew7bh_YMdc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn72@2020_6/2020/10/19/15-59-44-651_7432f500dc563e51.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1603123145994},{"createdTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","title":"TikTok removed videos by creators after a BBC investigation found them posting paid political content without disclosure","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/tiktok-removes-political-videos-after-bbc-says-didnt-disclose-payment-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f8d80dec21cfb0019468b1b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Trump TikTok\" height=\"1828\" width=\"3657\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A composite image of TikTok&#39;s logo and President Donald Trump.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Photo Illustration by Andrea Ronchini/NurPhoto via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>TikTok removed videos by creators who posted political content without disclosing it, the BBC reported.</li>\n<li>The BBC said the post were paid for by Bigtent Creative, a marketing company that is working to register young people to vote in the US presidential election.</li>\n<li>Many of the posts were apolitical and encouraged voter registration without favoring a candidate, the BBC said.</li>\n<li>However, some used their videos to criticize President Donald Trump, which place them under a different set of rules.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>TikTok removed videos after a BBC investigation found stars posting political videos without disclosing that they had been paid to do so by a marketing company.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/election-us-2020-54555798\">The BBC's Sophia Smith Galer reported</a> that Bigtent Creative, an agency which <a href=\"https://gizmodo.com/the-warren-meme-team-is-having-its-second-life-on-tikto-1845219816\">grew out of Elizabeth Warren's campaign in the Democratic primaries</a>, had been funding posts on the platform.</p><p>Smith Galer said that Bigtent-funded posts were a mixture of nonpartisan content encouraging people to register to vote, and some videos which explicitly encouraged people to vote against President Donald Trump.</p><p>The latter category would put the posts under different rules, since they would count as campaigning.</p><p>Bigtent Creative told the BBC that it gets non-partisan funding, and that the creators' videos should not have had anti-Trump sentiments.</p><p>The BBC reported that TikTok removed the videos after the BBC flagged them, citing the policy banning political advertising across the platform, and another requiring users to disclose when content has been paid for.</p><p>The videos got hundreds of thousands of views before they were removed, the BBC reported. The report did not name the creators, or how many were involved.</p><h2>Creators have to disclose paid endorsements</h2><p>A TikTok spokesperson told the BBC: \"These guidelines also apply to paid content by influencers, and we rely on influencers and marketers to follow FTC guidelines.</p><p>\"We remove paid influencer content that's not disclosed as such as we become aware of it and are now taking action on this.\"</p><p><a href=\"https://www.ftc.gov/tips-advice/business-center/guidance/ftcs-endorsement-guides-what-people-are-asking\">The US Federal Trade Commission (FTC) says</a> that paid endorsements on social media platforms like TikTok must be disclosed, typically by adding \"Sponsored\" or \"#ad\" to a post.</p><p>At least one of the creators told the BBC that they were unaware they were supposed to disclose the payment for the video.</p><p>Ysiad Ferreiras, Bigtent's CEO, said: \"Our work is not consistent with the platform's definition of an advertisement. Our creators make their own content as digital organisers, they're advocating for non-partisan action the same way paid canvassers and organisers working for non-profits do.\"</p><h2>Some of the videos were anti-Trump</h2><p>The FTC says that paid-for material that is non-partisan and encourages people to vote is not considered to be political and doesn't need to be disclosed, the BBC noted.</p><p>But some of the videos explicitly encouraged people to stop Trump from being elected.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f74cc2e74fe5b0018a8e4d0?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Trump debate.JPG\" height=\"1332\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>President Donald Trump in September 2020.\n<p class=\"copyright\">REUTERS/Brian Snyder/File Photo</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The BBC reported that the removed videos were often skits or memes, similar to the creators' regular output. They included a remix of the song \"WAP\" by Cardi B with the sound of Cardi B telling people to vote.</p><p>One of the series supported by Bigtent Creative was a number of high-profile creators forming a \"vote chain\" — where the creators made side-by-side videos called duets that promoted a voter registration link.</p><p>But it reported that some of the creators included messages against Trump, like: \"Trump is trying to ban TikTok again... can we please vote him out?\"</p><p>That chain is still on TikTok, but other funded chains that included creators calling Trump an \"angry Cheeto\" and \"orange Brussel sprout\" were removed, the BBC reported.</p><p>Bigtent Creative told the BBC that it has helped register 25,000 voters so far, with about half of those coming from TikTok.</p><figure></figure><p><a href=\"https://www.bigtentcreative.com/\">The company says on its website</a> \"We create new ways for GenZ and Millennials to engage in politics, meet them where they are, and influence their peers.\"</p><h2><a href=\"https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/election-us-2020-54555798\">Read the full BBC report &#62;&#62;</a></h2><div class=\"read-original\">Read the 2020 13:48:15 +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/5f8d80d69e9fe4001900a01d?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"01812128543f234719c33f0a7e79db01","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn35@2020_6/2020/10/19/15-59-40-170_f640b70e4c426192.webp","destWidth":3251,"destHeight":2438,"sourceBytes":162952,"destBytes":162952,"author":"sbaker@businessinsider.com (Sinéad Baker)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f8d80dec21cfb0019468b1b?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn87@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-46-239_62931dd5834fabe7.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f74cc2e74fe5b0018a8e4d0?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn15@2020_4/2020/10/19/15-59-41-076_ba081d8a555d3b34.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn56@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-47-418_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=L-7bx7EkD1o:STjZ44Md8bw:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn96@2020_1/2020/10/19/15-59-41-790_30fd590088a11e8a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=L-7bx7EkD1o:STjZ44Md8bw:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn40@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-45-818_be47c4c9a710e821.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn91@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-43-999_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=L-7bx7EkD1o:STjZ44Md8bw:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn72@2020_2/2020/10/19/15-59-50-604_cd89503e217ec890.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/L-7bx7EkD1o":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn59@2020_2/2020/10/19/15-59-49-088_480da027ca460609.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1603123145996},{"createdTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","title":"The Fox News Decision Desk director explains how races will be called on election night","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/fox-news-decision-desk-director-election-night-calling-races-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f8a02fdd260780019341f7a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"fox news building election\" height=\"1249\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A bust of Bart Simpson outside of Fox News headquarters in Midtown Manhattan.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Kevin Hagen/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Fox News rolled out a new methodology for calling elections ahead of the 2018 midterms, and the man at the helm says it will be much better equipped to deal with mail-in votes in 2020 compared to other models.</li>\n<li>Arnon Mishkin, director of the Fox News Decision Desk, did a Q&#38;A with Insider to discuss the challenges of calling an election on live TV like the American public has become accustomed to over the years.</li>\n<li>Mishkin said the surge of mail-in votes stemming from the coronavirus pandemic may make it difficult for outlets to call a winner on election night, but a wide enough margin between President Donald Trump and Democratic nominee Joe Biden may avert that problem.</li>\n<li>\"The timing of the call is going to be a function of how wide the margin is between the two candidates, and not just in the presidency, but also in the various other statewide races,\" Mishkin said.</li>\n<li>The new FOX News Voter Analysis system, Mishkin emphasized, is not designed to call elections faster, but simply more accurately.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Ahead of the 2018 midterm elections, Fox News decided to ditch the time-trodden model of using exit polls to help them call races as votes came in.</p><p>Instead, they took on a new approach: surveying upwards of 100,000 Americans ahead of Election Day to see where people are voting by mail or in person, and using that large dataset to make sense of the returns on election night.</p><p>Fox News Decision Desk Director Arnon Mishkin told Insider that the new method — the FOX News Voter Analysis system — was designed simply to call elections more accurately, not necessarily more quickly.</p><p>However, Mishkin noted that an advantage of this system during the coronavirus pandemic is its fine-tuning around mail-in voting, which is on pace to be far more widespread in 2020 than in past elections.</p><p>Below is a Q&#38;A with Mishkin where he explains how the network will approach calling the election amid rampant uncertainty and confusion around voting by mail.</p><p><em>This interview has been lightly edited for length and clarity.</em></p><p><strong>Jake Lahut:&#160;</strong>Could you start by walking me through what the previous system for calling elections was at Fox, and what this new one is doing differently?</p><p><strong>Arnon Mishkin:&#160;</strong>From the get-go, we were part of the National Election Pool (NEP) consortium, which basically ran the exit poll or purchased the exit poll — both the exit poll as well as the vote counts from the Associated Press. Following 2016, we were concerned about two things — and it wasn't just us, I think folks on the NEP were equally concerned. The two concerns were primarily the growth in the mail-in vote, and whether or not the exit poll was covering those people adequately enough. And the second was a tendency, for whatever reason, for there to be differential non-response — people who voted for one party seemed to be overrepresented in the sample than were represented when you actually count the vote. So there was a tendency for the results to skew.</p><p>We'd gotten sort of comfortable with realizing, well, if there's a lead here, that doesn't mean it's a lead because we know there's going to be a skew with the results. After 2016, the leadership at Fox said, \"We really need to come up with something new. The consortiums won't innovate, so we're pulling out, and you guys have to come up with something new.\"</p><p><strong>Lahut:&#160;</strong>Wow, so could you tell me what that initial meeting was like once you were tasked with coming up with a new model?</p><p><strong>Mishkin:&#160;</strong>We were sort of puzzled. Like, how are we going to do this without an exit poll? Then we met as a team here at Fox and developed what we thought would be a different way of approaching a \"voter x-ray,\" if you will — sort of understanding the voters. And we thought, let's do sort of a combination of a phone and internet poll across the country in the days before an election, which we thought would be the same tool for covering early voters, Election Day voters, and mail-in or absentee voters.</p><p>This was at a time, when we pulled out after 2016, when roughly a little over 40% of Americans were voting early or absentee. And we thought that the way the exit poll handled that was insufficient. So we developed a different approach.</p><p>We were very much told by our leadership here at Fox that we're not here to try and do something that's designed to give us a competitive advantage. We're trying to do something to improve the accuracy of the system. And we tried to explain that to our friends at NEP and at VAP. So we got the AP to join us, and we developed what we call the FoX News Voter Analysis and what they call AP VoteCast, working with the NORC (National Opinion Research Center) at the University of Chicago to come up with this combination phone and internet poll.</p><p><strong>Lahut:&#160;</strong>Could you tell me a little more about these surveys? Are they predicting the breakdown of the mail-in vote by offering a pattern of how those returns will split compared to the in-person votes?</p><p><strong>Mishkin: </strong>OK, so it's one standard tool. In every interview we find out if the voter is going to vote or has the voter voted, and also by what method they voted. So whether you're an in-person voter or a mail-in voter, you're taking the same survey. Based on that, and based on demographics of the country and various other things, it estimates — I wouldn't say predicts — what percentage are voting by mail, what percentage are voting in-person early, and what percentage are voting on Election Day. And those estimates are then also correlated with other estimates we get to make sure they're on track with what various other vendors provide on how many people have voted by mail.</p><p><strong>Lahut: </strong>Are there any rough benchmarks or circumstances where this model would allow you to call the race on election night or within 24 hours of the final votes being cast?</p><p><strong>Mishkin: </strong>The timing of the call is going to be a function of how wide the margin is between the two candidates, and not just in the presidency, but also in the various other statewide races. In the event that there's a wide margin, I think all of the networks are going to be able to make a call fairly quickly. In the event that the margin is much closer, you won't be able to make that call because you won't be able to see if there's a difference until later in the evening — and that's true in any election.&#160;</p><p>What makes this election more complex than most is that in most elections, mail-in voters differ a little from Election Day voters, but not that much in this election. For whatever reason, it has become almost an article of faith that Republicans should be voting on Election Day in person, and Democrats should be voting early or by mail. And so when you look at any or all the polls that have come out, it shows huge swings between how the mail-in vote is going to look and how the Election Day vote is going to look. That makes it far more complicated on election night, as we start seeing the vote to understand what exactly we are looking at.</p><p>We think that the combination of the three tools we'll have on election night to study the vote will give us a good idea of what's really happening, but it is much more complicated than it is historically. Those three tools, I should point out, are the Fox News Voter Analysis, the sample precincts that we'll be getting from the Associated Press, as well as the actual raw vote that you're getting county by county.</p><bi-shortcode id=\"expanded-coverage\" class=\"mceNonEditable\" data-source=\"insider-voter-guide\" data-show-header=\"true\">Expanded Coverage Module: +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/5f8a02ef9e9fe40019009e48?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"056772146ea31e499db71be4f974a30f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn19@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-38-930_974984e06653c2b2.webp","destWidth":1960,"destHeight":1470,"sourceBytes":98860,"destBytes":98860,"author":"insider@insider.com (Jake Lahut)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f8a02fdd260780019341f7a?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn48@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-47-611_cf8516de9fa48b6c.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn56@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-47-418_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=9Y5jLkbdL2E:EpS31Be1NOU:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn87@2020_4/2020/10/19/15-59-47-834_85d4caf98033b915.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=9Y5jLkbdL2E:EpS31Be1NOU:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn86@2020_2/2020/10/19/15-59-38-929_45d63957006938d8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn91@2020_5/2020/10/19/15-59-43-999_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=9Y5jLkbdL2E:EpS31Be1NOU:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn23@2020_3/2020/10/19/15-59-42-861_2ef590a01141705a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/9Y5jLkbdL2E":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn95@2020_3/2020/10/19/15-59-49-192_a90098a66c6a3fa0.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1603123145995},{"createdTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:06","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:06","title":"If you have a close relationship with your boss, you're more likely to lie, cheat, and make other unethical decisions on their behalf","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/good-relationship-with-your-boss-may-lead-to-unethical-behavior-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5e346d3e62fa814ead0ae616?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Woman talking to boss\" height=\"2200\" width=\"3300\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Employees with good relationships with their bosses may be more likely to make unethical decisions on their behalf.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Thomas Barwick/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Strong relationships with our bosses could lead us to make unethical decisions on their behalf, say leadership and business researchers Jennifer Jordan and Tim Vriend.</li>\n<li>People who are close to their managers are more likely to misrepresent the truth or withhold negative information about them as a sign of dedication.</li>\n<li>While great relationships at the workplace are beneficial for performance, morale, and job satisfaction, employers should always make it clear that lying, cheating, or any other unethical behavior will not be tolerated.&#160;</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>We generally believe that it is a good thing to have strong relationships with the people we work for and those we manage. Organizations and leaders do many things to cultivate this: retreats, one-on-one meetings, lunches, coaching — to name a few. But good relationships at work can also lead to unethical behavior.</p><p>In a <a href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/articles/10.3389/fpsyg.2020.01473/full\">recent study</a> with our colleagues Ramzi Said and Onne Janssen, we found that people who report stronger relationships with their bosses are more likely to engage in bad behavior on their behalf. This included things like misrepresenting the truth or withholding negative information about them to make their manager look good, even if it brought themselves no obvious or immediate benefit.</p><p>We conducted two studies. One was an experiment with more than 150 workers from the United States and the other a survey of more than 200 European employees. Across the two studies, we found that when employees believed that acting unethically helped their leader, they were more likely to do so if they had a strong relationship with them compared to when the relationship was weak.</p><p>We also discovered why this was happening. Workers acted unethically as a way to reciprocate or \"pay back\" their boss for their boss' dedication to them. This expectation was in no way made explicit by their boss. Instead, the workers were merely acting upon the universal norm of <a href=\"https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2017/09/170911122639.htm\">unspoken reciprocity</a>: If you do something good for me, I will do something good for you in return.</p><p>Similarly, we found that if people don't have a good relationship with their boss, they will do the opposite. They are more likely to engage in unethical behavior to support themselves, potentially at the expense of their boss.</p><p>The motivation was the same. Instead of reciprocating their good relationship with their boss, here people felt the need to get their own back on their bosses for having a bad relationship with them. They would be more willing to misrepresent the truth to make themselves look good or to withhold true but negative information about themselves to others in the organization.</p><p>Previous research tells us that this kind of behavior isn't limited to people's relationship with their immediate boss, but extends to the organization as a whole. <a href=\"https://psycnet.apa.org/record/2011-15805-006\">Other</a> <a href=\"https://psycnet.apa.org/record/2010-13313-013\">researchers</a> have found that employees who strongly identified with their organization were more likely to misrepresent the truth, withhold possibly damaging information about it, even if it meant acting against a customer's interests. Again, this is all out of a sense of paying back a company that they cared deeply about.</p><p>This work also suggests that when employees identify strongly with the organization they work for they might become blinded to any wrongdoing or unethical behavior because of their positive view of their workplace.</p><h2>Benefits to be had</h2><p>Research shows there are huge benefits for both companies and individuals when employees have good relationships with their managers, including a positive effect on their physical <a href=\"https://www.researchgate.net/publication/267764955_Leader-member_Exchange_LMX_and_Performance_A_Meta-Analytic_Review\">and mental well-being</a>. People who have strong relationships with their bosses also tend to perform better at work, spread goodwill further across the rest of the organization, and are more supportive of their peers — not to mention experiencing greater job satisfaction and developing a higher level of commitment to their company.</p><p>So bosses must put measures in place if they want to both cultivate strong bonds with the people who work for them and ensure that they act ethically and uphold strong company values. They must make it clear that lying, cheating, or doing anything else to protect their manager is neither allowed nor appreciated. Bosses must make it clear that they, nor anyone else in the company, sees this as a positive behavior that demonstrates loyalty.</p><p>And when bosses do observe this kind of behavior (even if it's on a small scale), they must immediately bring it to the worker's attention and perhaps even accompany it with a meaningful sanction in order to demonstrate the importance of not acting that way. Employees should also realize their potential to do the wrong thing in a misguided act of loyalty towards their boss — and avoid it.</p><p>Strong workplace relationships are always beneficial, particularly in the current climate of anxiety brought on by the COVID-19 pandemic. But, even so, these relationships should be the basis of more ethical, not unethical, behavior.<!-- Below is The Conversation's page counter tag. Please DO NOT REMOVE. --><!-- End of code. If you don't see any code above, please get new code from the Advanced tab after you click the republish button. The page counter does not collect any personal data. More info: https://theconversation.com/republishing-guidelines --></p><p><em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/jennifer-jordan-707356\">Jennifer Jordan</a>, professor of leadership &#38; organizational behavior, <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/international-institute-for-management-development-imd-3333\">International Institute for Management Development (IMD)</a> and <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/tim-vriend-1165261\">Tim Vriend</a>, assistant professor, economics and business, <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/university-of-groningen-1055\">University of Groningen</a></em></p><p>This article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license. executive as their new CEO, <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2020-10-16/buffett-inches-toward-wells-fargo-exit-as-scharf-sets-his-course?sref=Hny5JH2p\">according to Bloomberg</a>.</p><p>The billionaire investor and Berkshire Hathaway CEO <a href=\"https://www.ft.com/content/1ccb766e-565b-11e9-91f9-b6515a54c5b1\">told the Financial Times</a> last year that Wells Fargo, still reeling from its <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/wells-fargo-fake-accounts-scandal-impact-on-business-2016-12?r=US&IR=TIR=T\">fake-accounts scandal</a>, risked angering regulators if it hired its next boss from JPMorgan, Goldman Sachs, or another big bank.</p><p>\"They just have to come from someplace [outside Wells] and they shouldn't come from Wall Street,\" Buffett said.</p><p>\"There are plenty of good people to run it [from the Wall Street banks], but they are automatically going to draw the ire of a significant percentage of the Senate and the US House of Representatives, and that's just not smart,\" he added.</p><p><strong data-stringify-type=\"bold\"><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\">Read More:</em></strong><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\">&#160;</em><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-crash-3-nonsensical-narratives-dangerous-heights-peter-cecchini-2020-10\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-stringify-link=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-crash-3-nonsensical-narratives-dangerous-heights-peter-cecchini-2020-10\" data-sk=\"tooltip_parent\">An ex-Wall Street chief strategist lambasts 3 'nonsensical narratives' he says are pushing stocks to dangerous heights — and warns that the current rally is unsustainable</a></em></p><p>However, Wells Fargo went against the guidance of its biggest shareholder and appointed Charles Scharf, who worked at JPMorgan before running Visa and later BNY Mellon, in September of last year. It also agreed to let its new chief executive run the California bank from New York.</p><p>\"That's outrageous,\" Charlie Munger, Buffett's right-hand man and Berkshire's vice-chairman, said about the working arrangement in a <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2020-02-12/munger-says-wells-fargo-ceo-scharf-ought-to-be-in-san-francisco\">Bloomberg interview earlier this year</a>.</p><h2>Selling Wells</h2><p>Berkshire has been a Wells Fargo shareholder for <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-berkshire-hathaway-cuts-wells-fargo-stake-17-years-2020-9-1029566739#\">more than 30 years</a>, counting the bank among its five biggest holdings for most of that time. It owned more than 13% of the bank in 1994, and boasted more than 500 million shares worth over $27 billion in 2016.</p><p>However, it has cut its position by more than 60% this year to fewer than 140 million shares, giving it a roughly 3.3% position in the bank — its smallest percentage stake since 2003.</p><p>Buffett's company may have exited Wells Fargo entirely last quarter. Investors will find out when Berkshire's holdings as of 30 September are published in mid-November.</p><p><strong data-stringify-type=\"bold\"><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\">Read More:&#160;</em></strong><em data-stringify-type=\"italic\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-picks-to-buy-small-companies-recommendations-high-upside-rbc-2020-10\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" data-stringify-link=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-picks-to-buy-small-companies-recommendations-high-upside-rbc-2020-10\" data-sk=\"tooltip_parent\">Buy these 7 unheralded stocks right now for near-term upside of at least 25% as growth accelerates to a new level, RBC says</a></em></p><p>Berkshire did <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-sells-jpmorgan-wells-fargo-buys-barrick-gold-2020-8-1029506182#\">sell a bunch of financial stocks</a> in the second quarter including JPMorgan and Goldman Sachs. Wells Fargo also remains subject to a regulatory cap on its assets. The bank posted a rare quarterly loss and cut its dividend this year, providing several alternative reasons why Berkshire may have pared its position.</p><p>On the other hand, Buffett's company <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/warren-buffett-bank-of-america-buying-years-pay-off-experts-2020-8-1029477087#\">plowed $2.1 billion into Bank of America stock</a> over 12 straight trading days last quarter, suggesting he isn't bearish on the entire banking sector.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 23:59:05","updatedTime":"2020-10-19 23:59:05","title":"CVS is hiring 15,000 new staff to cope with an expected rise of COVID-19 cases and the potential rollout of a src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f8d8ae99e9fe4001900a02a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Luis Alvarez\" height=\"2685\" width=\"3580\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Luis Alvarez/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>CVS is hiring 15,000 new staff to prepare for an expected rise in COVID-19 cases during the colder months.</li>\n<li>This includes more than 10,000 pharmacy technicians, who will dispense medicines and administer COVID-19 tests.</li>\n<li>They may even be able administer vaccines, if the government authorizes proposed healthcare changes backed by CVS.</li>\n<li>CVS is also hiring \"several thousand\" full-time customer service roles that will be carried out remotely.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>CVS Health is hiring 15,000 new staff to cope with the expected rise in COVID-19 cases during fall and winter, <a href=\"https://cvshealth.com/news-and-insights/press-releases/cvs-health-to-hire-15000-across-the-us-in-fourth-quarter\">it announced Monday</a>.</p><p>The vacancies include more than 10,000 roles for full- and part-time licensed pharmacy technicians to dispense medicines and administer COVID-19 tests. The company wants to fill the positions \"as soon as possible.\"</p><p>Many of the roles are temporary, but could become permanent, the company added.</p><p>Technicians would also be able to administer COVID-19 vaccinations under the supervision of an immunization-certified pharmacist if state legislators authorizes proposed changes backed by CVS.</p><p>Since April, <a href=\"https://www.pharmacytimes.com/news/changes-in-practice-scope-of-pharmacy-technicians-needed-to-address-community-needs-during-covid-19-outbreak\">pharmacists have called on US legislators to introduce this policy</a>, which the state of&#160;<a href=\"https://news3lv.com/news/local/sisolak-nevada-emergency-covid-19-vaccine\">Nevada did in September</a>. CVS says the change would help more people get vaccinated.</p><p>CVS said the new hires would \"help the company continue to respond to the needs of communities across the country during the fall and winter months when the incidences of COVID-19 and the flu are expected to increase.\"</p><p>CVS usually recruits new staff every flu season, but Lisa Bisaccia, the company's chief human resources officer, said it expects \"a much greater need for trained pharmacy technicians this year given the continued presence of COVID-19 in our communities.\"</p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/companies-making-fast-home-coronavirus-tests-sherlock-mammoth-e25-2020-10\">Meet the 11 companies racing to make coronavirus tests that cost as little as $5 and deliver results in under 15 minutes</a></em></p><p>CVS is also recruiting pharmacists, nurses, nurse practitioners, physician assistants, and distribution center employees.</p><p>It has also opened \"several thousand\" full-time remote customer service roles. These have a flexible schedule, the company said, which makes them suitable for people returning to work during the pandemic.</p><p>Candidates can <a href=\"https://jobs.cvshealth.com/Call-Center-Job-Near-Me\">try the job out online</a> before they apply.</p><p>CVS Health opened the first large-scale COVID-19 testing sites in the US in April, and has administered <a href=\"https://cvshealth.com/news-and-insights/press-releases/cvs-health-to-partner-with-long-term-care-facilities-when-covid-19\">more than 5 million tests</a>.</p><p>The company aims to provide around approximately 5 million COVID-19 tests per month, it added, and is currently offering tests <a href=\"https://cvshealth.com/news-and-insights/press-releases/cvs-health-to-partner-with-long-term-care-facilities-when-covid-19\">at nearly 4,000 drive-thru pharmacies</a>.</p><p>To meet these needs, CVS has hired nearly 80,000 full-time, part-time and temporary employees so far, a spokesperson told <a href=\"https://www.nbcnews.com/business/business-news/cvs-hire-thousands-pharmacy-techs-it-prepares-more-covid-19-n1243860\">NBC News</a>.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on The annual membership costs $59.95 (or $5/month) and is billed once yearly. If you opt to do a monthly membership instead, you'll pay $9.95 per month.&#160;</p><p>You'll get free shipping on your first order over $25, plus all orders $49 or more.&#160;Thrive Market currently ships to all contiguous US states.</p><h2><strong>How it works:</strong></h2><ul><li><strong><a href=\"https://www.anrdoezrs.net/links/6415797/type/dlg/https://thrivemarket.com/web/membership/welcome\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener nofollow sponsored nofollow sponsored nofollow sponsored nofollow sponsored\">Sign up for free</a></strong>: You can browse the catalog, see member saving, and receive 25% off your first purchase.</li><li><strong><a href=\"https://www.anrdoezrs.net/links/6415797/type/dlg/https://thrivemarket.com/how-it-works\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"nofollow noopener sponsored nofollow sponsored\">Start a free 30-day trial</a></strong><a href=\"https://www.anrdoezrs.net/links/6415797/type/dlg/https://thrivemarket.com/how-it-works\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"nofollow noopener sponsored\"></a>: You can start a free 30-day membership trial with your first purchase on Thrive Market. Cancel anytime.</li><li><strong>Join as a member:</strong> After your trial, you can sign up for a paid membership. If you do, you're also sponsoring a free membership for a low-income family.&#160;</li></ul><p>You can shop Thrive Market category, by values (ie. gluten-free, paleo, raw, vegan, etc.), or by current <a href=\"http://www.anrdoezrs.net/links/6415797/type/dlg/sid/biipgf031418besthikingsocks/https://thrivemarket.com/deals\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener nofollow sponsored nofollow sponsored\">deals</a>.&#160;</p><h2>What can you buy at Thrive Market?</h2><p>Thrive Market carries 6,000+ healthy products, and you can shop <a href=\"http://www.anrdoezrs.net/links/6415797/type/dlg/sid/biipgf031418besthikingsocks/https://thrivemarket.com/all-categories\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener nofollow sponsored nofollow sponsored\">hundreds of categories</a>. rel=\"noopener nofollow sponsored\">Califa</a>, <a
